<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d4c48ba8-5ef7-4343-a850-97141c3fe7fd'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer187200239714619286">At one magical instant in your early childhood, the page of a book<b>—</b>that string of confused, alien ciphers<b>—</b>shivered into meaning. Words spoke to you, gave up their secrets; at that moment, whole universes opened. You became, irrevocably, a reader. Noted essayist Alberto Manguel moves from this essential moment to explore the 6000-year-old conversation between words and that</span>
  <span id="freeText187200239714619286" style="display:none">At one magical instant in your early childhood, the page of a book<b>—</b>that string of confused, alien ciphers<b>—</b>shivered into meaning. Words spoke to you, gave up their secrets; at that moment, whole universes opened. You became, irrevocably, a reader. Noted essayist Alberto Manguel moves from this essential moment to explore the 6000-year-old conversation between words and that magician without whom the book would be a lifeless object: the reader. Manguel lingers over reading as seduction, as rebellion, as obsession, and goes on to trace the never-before-told story of the reader's progress from clay tablet to scroll, codex to CD-ROM.</span>
  <a data-text-id="187200239714619286" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_113259566" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113259566" />
    <a title="Lobstergirl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl"><img alt="Lobstergirl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380256801p2/1910836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/113259566?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lobstergirl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lobstergirl" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl">Lobstergirl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1910836-lobstergirl?shelf=books-on-books">books-on-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer113259566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10033913100100072536">Though this is not a comprehensive history of reading, being only 319 pages of text excluding notes and index, it certainly feels like it is. Dense with information, weighty with erudition, wordy with words, it covers the history of reading lying down, sitting up, in one's bedchamber, with and without eyeglasses, through translation, silently and aloud, alone and in groups. The history of tablets, scrolls, and codices is covered. The development of libraries and cataloging gets ample shrift. The</span>
  <span id="freeText10033913100100072536" style="display:none">Though this is not a comprehensive history of reading, being only 319 pages of text excluding notes and index, it certainly feels like it is. Dense with information, weighty with erudition, wordy with words, it covers the history of reading lying down, sitting up, in one's bedchamber, with and without eyeglasses, through translation, silently and aloud, alone and in groups. The history of tablets, scrolls, and codices is covered. The development of libraries and cataloging gets ample shrift. The sizes and shapes of books throughout history are examined. Book theft, book banning, book burning all get their due. Talmudic and exegetical reading are dwelt on. Pretty much the only reading I felt was underaddressed was the sylvan nudist Kabbalist-Satanic Braille reading groups of the 13th century Faroe Islanders.<br /><br />For the first quarter of the book, I felt annoyed. It seemed like a ponderous vanity project, as Manguel impressed the vast span of his knowledge on the reader. I absorbed like a goose being stuffed for a <i>pâté de foie gras</i>. When I finally let go of my irritation and allowed the narrative to wash over me, I began to enjoy the book. And of course, it enabled me to add many questions to the Neverending Quiz™.<br /><br />The book is packed with illustrations, woodcuts, engravings, and photos. Two photos struck me. One shows a bombed out library in 1940 London. The ceiling has collapsed, the floor is kneedeep in debris. Yet the bookshelves lining the room still stand, and three men, in hats and overcoats to protect them from the October air, examine the books. One has his hand outstretched to pick, another is reading an open book. It's almost as if it's just another day at the library.<br /><br />In the second, a slave woman stands in the doorway of her cabin in 1856 Aiken, South Carolina. Next to her a slave girl stands reading a book, presumably aloud, to a group of 5 or 6 children of about kindergarten age sitting in a circle on the ground. Four of the children are slave and wearing what are obviously 19th century clothes; the fifth, with his back to us in three-quarter profile, is white. He wears a timeless checkered shirt and has a short haircut that would be equally at home in 1940 or 2010. He looks so much like a little boy I know that I kept staring at the photo and its remarkable interracial story time. The photo is captioned "A rare photo of a slave reading." Like all interesting photos, it left me wanting to know more. Who were these children? Did this slaveowner encourage reading? Who was he? Did they have story time every day? How did the mixed story time of these children affect their ideas about race? Who did they grow up to be? I might have to keep reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10033913100100072536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_96070541" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96070541" />
    <a title="Al Bità" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1533440-al-bit"><img alt="Al Bità" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483528143p2/1533440.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96070541?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Al Bità" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Al Bità" href="/user/show/1533440-al-bit">Al Bità</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96070541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14313520668757493805">For some reason I found this work quite disappointing — perhaps because the title promises more than the book delivers? The book comes across more as an academic 'showing-off' of the author's extensive reading habits. It quickly becomes a series of articles that discuss various aspects of writing, printing, book publishing, what it means to 'read', or to 'be read to', book burnings, book collecting, etc.<br/><br/>All of these are interesting subjects in themselves, and often the sections include fascinati</span>
  <span id="freeText14313520668757493805" style="display:none">For some reason I found this work quite disappointing — perhaps because the title promises more than the book delivers? The book comes across more as an academic 'showing-off' of the author's extensive reading habits. It quickly becomes a series of articles that discuss various aspects of writing, printing, book publishing, what it means to 'read', or to 'be read to', book burnings, book collecting, etc.<br /><br />All of these are interesting subjects in themselves, and often the sections include fascinating historical matters — and yes, academically speaking, they can all be associated with 'reading' in one way or another... But perhaps a better title would have been "A History of Books'. But even that is misleading: in the final section called 'Endpaper Pages' the author begins to write about wanting to read a book called 'The History of Reading' (note the definite article difference to the indefinite article used for this book itself) which he describes, but which hasn't been written yet. Shades of Jorge Luis Borges?!<br /><br />So what is this book about? Books, writing, publishing, 'reading', contributions to knowledge, real as opposed to imagined books, etc. — in a sense it is all of these, and in another sense, none of them. It strikes me as an academic conceit, amusing to a certain degree, academically detached in another. I suspect the author must have enjoyed researching and writing this work, but for the ordinary reader it seems to me to be unnecessarily complex and obfuscatory on the subject of 'reading': lots of smoke, little heat, and not much illumination.<br /><br />At best this book has curiosity value, and may appeal on that level.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14313520668757493805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50864055" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16621731111238410223">A History of Reading is a rich and wonderful book - a treasury of knowledge, stories and illustrations - that takes us on an unforgettable journey. Infinitely engaging and amusing, a sweeping exploration of what it means to be a reader of books, A History of Reading is a brilliant reminder of why we cherish the act of reading - despite distractions from the Inquisition to the lures of cyberspace.<br/>Alberto Manguel reminds us that readers live in books as well as among them: we find our own stories </span>
  <span id="freeText16621731111238410223" style="display:none">A History of Reading is a rich and wonderful book - a treasury of knowledge, stories and illustrations - that takes us on an unforgettable journey. Infinitely engaging and amusing, a sweeping exploration of what it means to be a reader of books, A History of Reading is a brilliant reminder of why we cherish the act of reading - despite distractions from the Inquisition to the lures of cyberspace.<br />Alberto Manguel reminds us that readers live in books as well as among them: we find our own stories in books and traces of our lives. With every book that matters to us, from childhood into old age, we find our lives refreshed. He shows us what happens when we read; who we become; and how reading teaches us to live " - from the inside jacket of the first edition of A History of Reading.<br /><br />A classic justification of why people read (and will always read.) His combination use of personal stories, historical references and illustrations do make this book not only enjoyable but quite informative. It is a must read for anybody who reads books. And a must read for the "ogre under the bridge" who foolishly thinks books no longer matter.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4132278688270966752">2 stars = it was okay. A shame, as this was only okay and I thought it should have been great. There was a wealth of information about the history of reading, though none of it felt particularly well organised. I enjoyed some parts, but then found whole sections very dry and laborious to get through. I preferred the paragraphs about famous authors and quotes from their work, which led to me keeping notes of other books I'd like to read. Whereas I was not particularly interested in Manguel's own </span>
  <span id="freeText4132278688270966752" style="display:none">2 stars = it was okay. A shame, as this was only okay and I thought it should have been great. There was a wealth of information about the history of reading, though none of it felt particularly well organised. I enjoyed some parts, but then found whole sections very dry and laborious to get through. I preferred the paragraphs about famous authors and quotes from their work, which led to me keeping notes of other books I'd like to read. Whereas I was not particularly interested in Manguel's own history of reading, which I thought this was at times. Overall, though I found it greatly researched I didn't find it very well written. I'm not sure if this was a lack of expression through the translation, or just that I didn't like the writing style, but ultimately I was relieved to reach the end. </span>

<div id="review_48047663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48047663" />
    <a title="M. D.  Hudson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1478575-m-d-hudson"><img alt="M. D.  Hudson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593964179p2/1478575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48047663?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M. D.  Hudson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M. D.  Hudson" href="/user/show/1478575-m-d-hudson">M. D.  Hudson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48047663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17975807626132122974">The problem with all books about reading is that they tend to get somewhat self-regarding, too self-conscious. As for this one, I stopped reading this about halfway through about a year ago. Sometimes Manguel's Joy of Reading passages got too purple for me, despite the interesting facts here and there. There's interesting stuff beneath the goosh, however, and when the book reemerged from my bedside pile, I found the second half quite enjoyable. Still, too much frosting, not enough cake. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1971677485" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1971677485" />
    <a title="Daniela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62181206-daniela"><img alt="Daniela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527432849p2/62181206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1971677485?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniela" href="/user/show/62181206-daniela">Daniela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1971677485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9542194522041675108">Reading is, and has been for a long time, a vital part of my life. As Manguel points out, it is a skill that once learned can't be unlearned. And, even more dangerously, it rapidly becomes automatic. When you can read, you just do it. Your brain translates those symbols - the letters - into meanings in an unavoidable process. That is, you can't stop yourself from reading (if you know the language, of course) anymore you can't stop yourself from hearing. <br/><br/>Manguel is brilliant at ascribing deepfelt</span>
  <span id="freeText9542194522041675108" style="display:none">Reading is, and has been for a long time, a vital part of my life. As Manguel points out, it is a skill that once learned can't be unlearned. And, even more dangerously, it rapidly becomes automatic. When you can read, you just do it. Your brain translates those symbols - the letters - into meanings in an unavoidable process. That is, you can't stop yourself from reading (if you know the language, of course) anymore you can't stop yourself from hearing. <br /><br />Manguel is brilliant at ascribing deepfelt meanings to the act of reading and to the love of books that comes as a consequence. For him, reading contains all the multitudes of life. It is dangerous, it is comforting, it is thrilling, it is boring, it is fun, it is instructive, it is educational, it is subversive. One could say, borrowing from Dr. Jonhson, that when a man is tired of books he is tired of life. For there is in books all that life can afford. This is perhaps Manguel's most important point. It destroys the idea that people who love books and who read a lot are merely trying to find replacements for a life they can't - or refuse - to have. A History of Reading shows precisely the opposite. A love of reading is a love of life. In all its multitudes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9542194522041675108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_194474655" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194474655" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194474655?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2016">read2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=readingmayx">readingmayx</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194474655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12160077903952482773">"I wanted to live among books."<br/><br/>This is one of the better books on reading and books that I've read by Manguel (and it's a topic he loves to write about) because it is not merely a compilation of essays he has written elsewhere or a list of books - he is writing about the history of reading within civilization and within his life. I found a lot of great topics for my reading class (which is why I read it) and learned more about a lot of things. I did not know that he used to read out loud to the </span>
  <span id="freeText12160077903952482773" style="display:none">"I wanted to live among books."<br /><br />This is one of the better books on reading and books that I've read by Manguel (and it's a topic he loves to write about) because it is not merely a compilation of essays he has written elsewhere or a list of books - he is writing about the history of reading within civilization and within his life. I found a lot of great topics for my reading class (which is why I read it) and learned more about a lot of things. I did not know that he used to read out loud to the aging Borges, and of course now he holds his same position in the National Library of Argentina. <br /></span>

<div id="review_1189506620" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1189506620" />
    <a title="Abby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6262367-abby"><img alt="Abby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1477240488p2/6262367.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1189506620?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abby" href="/user/show/6262367-abby">Abby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6262367-abby?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6262367-abby?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6262367-abby?shelf=lit-theory">lit-theory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1189506620" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15703712583972378190"><blockquote>“As I build pile after pile of familiar volumes (I recognize some by their colour, others by their shape, many by a detail on the jackets whose titles I try to read upside down or at an odd angle) I wonder, as I have wondered every other time, why I keep so many books that I know I will not read again. I tell myself that, every time I get rid of a book, I find a few days later that this is precisely the book I’m looking for. I tell myself that there are no books (or very, very few) in which I ha</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText15703712583972378190" style="display:none"><blockquote>“As I build pile after pile of familiar volumes (I recognize some by their colour, others by their shape, many by a detail on the jackets whose titles I try to read upside down or at an odd angle) I wonder, as I have wondered every other time, why I keep so many books that I know I will not read again. I tell myself that, every time I get rid of a book, I find a few days later that this is precisely the book I’m looking for. I tell myself that there are no books (or very, very few) in which I have found nothing at all to interest me. I tell myself that I’ve brought them into my house for a reason in the first place, and that this reason may hold good again in the future. I invoke excuses of thoroughness, of scarcity, of faint scholarship. But I know that the main reason I hold onto this ever-increasing hoard is a sort of voluptuous greed. I enjoy the sight of my crowded bookshelves, full of more or less familiar names. I delight in knowing that I’m surrounded by a sort of inventory of my life, with intimations of my future. I like discovering, in almost forgotten volumes, traces of the reader I once was—scribbles, bus tickets, scraps of paper with mysterious names and numbers, the occasional date and place on the book’s flyleaf which take me back to a certain café, a distant hotel room, a faraway summer so long ago. I could, if I had to, abandon these books of mine and begin again, somewhere else; I have done so before, several times, out of necessity. But then I have also had to acknowledge a grave, irreparable loss. I know that something dies when I give up my books, and that my memory keeps going back to them with mournful nostalgia. And now, with the years, my memory can recall less and less, and seems to me like a looted library: many of the rooms have been closed, and in the ones still open for consultation there are huge gaps on the shelves. I pull out one of the remaining books and see that several of its pages have been torn out by vandals. The more decrepit my memory becomes, the more I wish to protect this repository of what I’ve read, this collection of textures and voices and scents. Possessing these books has become all-important to me, because I’ve become jealous of the past.”</blockquote><br /><br />Just the kind of topical history that I like: abandons the straight chronological depiction and instead breaks up the subject into thematic chapters (The Missing First Page, Private Reading, The Symbolic Reader, Forbidden Reading). Alberto Manguel is a skillful writer and (obviously) devoted reader, who sounds like he would be a dream dinner guest. (A tiny anecdote: At 16, while living in Buenos Aires, he met Borges in the bookstore where Manguel was working as a teen, and Borges asked him if he would come over in the evenings to read to him, which he did faithfully for two years. Cue intense envy.) A history for readers, for sure, and much more delightful and enjoyable than I had even expected.<br /><br /><blockquote>“I have sometimes dreamt that when the Day of Judgment dawns and the great conquerors and lawyers and statesmen come to receive their rewards—their crowns, their laurels, their names carved indelibly upon imperishable marble—the Almighty will turn to Peter and will say, not without a certain envy when He sees us coming with our books under our arms, ‘Look, these need no reward. We have nothing to give them. They have loved reading.’”</blockquote> — Virginia Woolf, “How Should One Read a Book?” <i>The Common Reader 2</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="15703712583972378190" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1904982462" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1904982462" />
    <a title="Nikki Stafford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2468245-nikki-stafford"><img alt="Nikki Stafford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442957627p2/2468245.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1904982462?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nikki Stafford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nikki Stafford" href="/user/show/2468245-nikki-stafford">Nikki Stafford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1904982462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3931533312871416895">I bought this book over 20 years ago, and it's sat on my shelf all these years and I finally picked it up and read it. And I'm really glad I did. This is one of those books that alters the way you read books from that point forward. He talks about how readers bring something to every book — the writer writes one book, but it's the reader who imagines whatever bedroom is in their head, or pictures the lead character a certain way. He talks about how historically readers — and especially bespectac</span>
  <span id="freeText3931533312871416895" style="display:none">I bought this book over 20 years ago, and it's sat on my shelf all these years and I finally picked it up and read it. And I'm really glad I did. This is one of those books that alters the way you read books from that point forward. He talks about how readers bring something to every book — the writer writes one book, but it's the reader who imagines whatever bedroom is in their head, or pictures the lead character a certain way. He talks about how historically readers — and especially bespectacled ones — have been either revered or made fun of. There are many moments where he goes back to the pre-Christian era to discuss early writing and reading, and those sections can be relatively dry. My favourite moments were when he talked about his own experiences as a reader, and I wish he'd included more of those. I also realized I'm more interested in modern history than ancient history, for when he was discussing forbidden books and how African-American slaves were hiding books or the very fact they could read, I was fascinated. One thing I found amusing was just how much technology has changed in the 20 years since he wrote this book: he mentions how entire libraries could now be available on a set of CD-ROMS and how extraordinary that is. Of course, now they could all be on a tiny thumb drive. Due to the date there's no mention of smartphones or the internet, two things that have definitely changed the way we read. But I found that oddly refreshing, that for once we could talk about books without really delving into today's technology. This is a really enjoyable read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3931533312871416895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_571046088" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571046088" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/445677-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487280374p2/445677.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/571046088?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/445677-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer571046088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3510769690870802694">This is the second book I have read by Alberto Manguel. The first, "The Library at Night," was magnificent, and it left me eager to enjoy more of his erudite prose and wide-ranging stories of scholars and enthusiasts within the literary and humanist tradition. In "A History of Reading" the chapters cover eclectic topics, providing not a chronological but a conceptual history of reading and supporting stories of readers. I found myself wanting to spend time ruminating on each chapter, so I found </span>
  <span id="freeText3510769690870802694" style="display:none">This is the second book I have read by Alberto Manguel. The first, "The Library at Night," was magnificent, and it left me eager to enjoy more of his erudite prose and wide-ranging stories of scholars and enthusiasts within the literary and humanist tradition. In "A History of Reading" the chapters cover eclectic topics, providing not a chronological but a conceptual history of reading and supporting stories of readers. I found myself wanting to spend time ruminating on each chapter, so I found myself not eager to rush, but rather to contemplate and think more deeply. For me the history of reading, and of the developments from the scroll to the codex to the hypertext, parallel and synapse with the history of ideas of knowledge, of society, of the religious, humanist, and scientific enterprises - in short, for me to be a reader is fundamental to being fully human, and I intend this thought broadly and in a non-elitist context. We understand ourselves through what we read. For me this book continued and extended this dialogue in a most pleasant manner.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3510769690870802694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5172494826837100925" style="display:none">Perhaps this is the best way to state how much I loved this book, I just finished an ebook edition from my library and purchased a hard copy immediately for myself. There is just too much amazing information contained within to have it be an ebook. I have numerous digital highlights, but this is the sort of book that warrants writing in margins, notes, dog ears, and post-it tags, hence again why I have a copy in the mail. As a teacher of Ancient Civ there is so much fascinating content about Mesopotamia and Greece, as a feminist so many powerful but little known women (who knew Sargon the Great's daughter, Princess Enheduanna was the first recorded female writer!?!) are discussed, and as bibliophile there is so much to say about the power of written words. It is a must have on any bookshelf!</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2868821386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7875187124154566883">The author of a 'Night at the library' is a reader. To him reading seems to be an essential facet of life - only he does it not to survive but live. This shows in his history of reading. He offers nuggets from history and the worlds of texts and reading to illuminate particular elements of reading - like reading quiet, reading alone, judging a person by the book's cover, and forbidden reading. As if that were not enough he tops it with anecdotes; of growing up with books and even more fantastic,</span>
  <span id="freeText7875187124154566883" style="display:none">The author of a 'Night at the library' is a reader. To him reading seems to be an essential facet of life - only he does it not to survive but live. This shows in his history of reading. He offers nuggets from history and the worlds of texts and reading to illuminate particular elements of reading - like reading quiet, reading alone, judging a person by the book's cover, and forbidden reading. As if that were not enough he tops it with anecdotes; of growing up with books and even more fantastic, with Borges. He answers questions that are not profound but fun to think about but ignored (because of their mundaneness). Finally the book is not even a history in the usual sense of the word. There is no chronology but there are ideas and lots and lots of words.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7875187124154566883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_307111147" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/307111147" />
    <a title="1.1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3728503-1-1"><img alt="1.1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307338551p2/3728503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/307111147?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="1.1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="1.1" href="/user/show/3728503-1-1">1.1</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer307111147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5949720043438334090">I am guilty lately of overusing the term 'interesting' to refer to books in quick, discouraged reviews. Clearly I've not been passionate enough about reviewing properly. I sincerely doubt things will change unless I get back in the habit of keeping notes and page numbers for quotes and all that work business, which I feel detract from the pleasures of reading.<br/><br/>Alberto Manguel does pretty much what an enshrined member of the establishment would do: a learned summary of reading, in purple prose, wi</span>
  <span id="freeText5949720043438334090" style="display:none">I am guilty lately of overusing the term 'interesting' to refer to books in quick, discouraged reviews. Clearly I've not been passionate enough about reviewing properly. I sincerely doubt things will change unless I get back in the habit of keeping notes and page numbers for quotes and all that work business, which I feel detract from the pleasures of reading.<br /><br />Alberto Manguel does pretty much what an enshrined member of the establishment would do: a learned summary of reading, in purple prose, with very interesting anecdotes. He also glosses over the negative aspects of reading: the tracts and ideological messes, the elitism, the mountains of paper wasted on trivial hacks, dangerous idiots, and dull nonsense. The people permanently crippled by print are mostly ignored, too.<br /><br />But Alberto had a cool, dangerous, sickly and cloistered life. He doesn't even pause to tell us what gender his lover was – ah but of course, the book is 'a' history, and not his history of reading. If only he stuck to that course a little more often. Still, he brings his own experiences into the book often, and mostly they add zest to the pages, but for all that the title remains slightly deceptive.<br /><br />Definitely worth reading. It can be a quick, casual read, and is not entirely lacking in rigor.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5949720043438334090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_325592013" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/325592013" />
    <a title="Graychin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1013182-graychin"><img alt="Graychin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597960333p2/1013182.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/325592013?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Graychin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Graychin" href="/user/show/1013182-graychin">Graychin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer325592013" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13168007988305935588">I’m half ashamed of enjoying Manguel as much as I do. I cringe at movies about movies, and (for the most part) can’t stand listening to artists talk about art. To read with pleasure about the pleasures of reading, therefore, makes me feel a bit dirty, like I’m indulging in something vaguely illicit. <br/><br/>Most of the time (when he’s not reminiscing about reading to the blind Borges as an adolescent) Manguel is smart enough to make you feel that you’re at least being educated. <i>A History of Reading</i> is p</span>
  <span id="freeText13168007988305935588" style="display:none">I’m half ashamed of enjoying Manguel as much as I do. I cringe at movies about movies, and (for the most part) can’t stand listening to artists talk about art. To read with pleasure about the pleasures of reading, therefore, makes me feel a bit dirty, like I’m indulging in something vaguely illicit. <br /><br />Most of the time (when he’s not reminiscing about reading to the blind Borges as an adolescent) Manguel is smart enough to make you feel that you’re at least being educated. <i>A History of Reading</i> is perhaps a little stronger, in that respect, than <i>The Library at Night</i>.<br /><br />And when it comes down to it, where else except in an Alberto Manguel book am I going to learn that <i>the Grand Vizier of Persia, Abdul Kassem Ismael, in order not to part with his collection of 117,000 volumes when travelling, had them carried by a caravan of four hundred camels trained to walk in alphabetical order</i>? <br /><br />Or where else am I going to learn that <i>the ancient Mesopotamians believed birds to be sacred because their footsteps on wet clay left marks that resembled cuneiform writing, and imagined that, if they could decipher the confusion of those signs, they would know what the gods were thinking</i>?<br /><br />How can I ever have lived contented without knowing these things?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13168007988305935588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26347286" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26347286" />
    <a title="Lauren " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1291915-lauren"><img alt="Lauren " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604235645p2/1291915.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26347286?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren " href="/user/show/1291915-lauren">Lauren </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=general-nonfiction">general-nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26347286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11730685873361345109">Alberto Manguel spent a good deal of time "crossing his t's and dotting his i's" in this book. The research is solid, and serves well as a spring board into the deeper book history and history of scholarship. The chapters are stand-alone vignettes, not necessarily following a chronological model throughout the book.<br/><br/>Manguel also ventures into auxiliary areas of interest, like the history of eyeglasses, and history of printing. _The History of Reading_ was a real joy to read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/769356-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479423940p2/769356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3548642528?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/769356-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3548642528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18302150726304167213">Widely read, widely traveled, Alberto Manguel offers a series of brief historical essays on various aspects of reading from around the world over the last six millennia. <br/><br/>Every chapter contains stimulating tidbits. We read of the creative but (to us) farfetched theories the ancients had on how people were actually able to decipher markings on a stone or piece of papyrus. One of the world’s most notorious rare book thieves, we learn, was Count Guglielmo Libri who permanently checked out hundreds o</span>
  <span id="freeText18302150726304167213" style="display:none">Widely read, widely traveled, Alberto Manguel offers a series of brief historical essays on various aspects of reading from around the world over the last six millennia. <br /><br />Every chapter contains stimulating tidbits. We read of the creative but (to us) farfetched theories the ancients had on how people were actually able to decipher markings on a stone or piece of papyrus. One of the world’s most notorious rare book thieves, we learn, was Count Guglielmo Libri who permanently checked out hundreds of items from special library collections throughout Europe in the nineteen century. <br /><br />Aristocratic women in medieval Japan were forced to develop their own literature since they were so severely cut off from the world. In the first century AD Roman Pliny the Younger found reason to castigate and praise authors who read (the better ones would perform) their latest creations to a gathering of friends and others. <br /> <br />I knew of the ways monks would read the Bible: lectio, meditatio, oratio and contemplation. Manguel notes that the Ashkenazi Talmudic scholars also used a different set of four simultaneous levels of reading -- the “<i>Pshat</i> or literal sense, <i>Remez</i> or limited meeting, <i>Drash</i> or rational elaboration and <i>Sod</i> or occult, secret, mystical meaning” (p. 90).<br /><br />The author also intermixes some of his own experiences into the book. When Manguel told about how he read, while a teenager, to the blind Jorge Luis Borges for several years, I found myself in a rare state of jealousy. How fascinating to have been able to hear Borges’s comments on Kipling, Keats, Milton, Joyce, Estrada, Papini, Lovecraft, Waugh, and more! Understandably, Manguel describes this task as a “happy captivity," as is this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18302150726304167213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2169037823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3607326695038930524">Manguel, one imagines him writing in library windows that overlook trees planted in old cities, shares moments from the history of reading in a free form structure that goes forwards, backwards, and, sometimes, upwards. Readers who come to this history looking for an eclectic, dreamy few hundred pages of prose on reading may enjoy it more than I did. Please note, however, that if someone had told me that this is an eclectic, dreamy few hundred pages of prose on reading, I would have rushed out t</span>
  <span id="freeText3607326695038930524" style="display:none">Manguel, one imagines him writing in library windows that overlook trees planted in old cities, shares moments from the history of reading in a free form structure that goes forwards, backwards, and, sometimes, upwards. Readers who come to this history looking for an eclectic, dreamy few hundred pages of prose on reading may enjoy it more than I did. Please note, however, that if someone had told me that this is an eclectic, dreamy few hundred pages of prose on reading, I would have rushed out the door to buy it. In fact, I did, and only now do I call to mind an injunction passed down to us from wise ancients: know thyself. I respect both the wide array of facts written here and the clear care Manguel has for reading, but, upon finishing the final pages, I'm mostly relieved to have rid myself of the guilt I've long felt for not having yet read <i>A History of Reading</i>.</span>

<div id="review_165155840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/165155840" />
    <a title="Claire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5362894-claire"><img alt="Claire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434918031p2/5362894.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/165155840?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire" href="/user/show/5362894-claire">Claire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5362894-claire?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer165155840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7752421874644591554">So I was really super excited to start this book and I have to admit I was slightly disappointed.<br/><br/>First of all, the book did start off on a great foot. The author gave his own personal history of how he started reading and why he fell in love with the art (yes, I consider reading an art. If you read this book you’ll understand why.) which I could totally identify with, but really it descended into organized chaos after that.<br/><br/>The book is divided into a few different sections and those sections are </span>
  <span id="freeText7752421874644591554" style="display:none">So I was really super excited to start this book and I have to admit I was slightly disappointed.<br /><br />First of all, the book did start off on a great foot. The author gave his own personal history of how he started reading and why he fell in love with the art (yes, I consider reading an art. If you read this book you’ll understand why.) which I could totally identify with, but really it descended into organized chaos after that.<br /><br />The book is divided into a few different sections and those sections are divided into chapters, which are in chronological order. I personally didn’t like this organization. I would have preferred the chapters just to be in chronological order, because I kept getting bounced back and forth between time periods, which really kept me from really getting into the book.<br /><br />And I know this is ironic, but this book is really wordy. I know what you’re thinking: “Duh, its a book on reading, of course its wordy. What are you stupid? Don’t you know how reading works?” But honestly, it was a little much, even for a voracious reader like myself. Some people probably enjoy wordy books, but I’d rather my literature just get straight to the point.<br /><br />But onto the positives. I actually learned a lot about the history of the book and the history of reading. I also really liked how the author intertwined his own experiences into the book to make it more personal. I really was able to identify with Manguel. He’s a reader after my own heart. The book also included a lot of pictures, which besides from being something pretty to look at (because who doesn’t love pictures?) they were helpful when Manguel referenced certain pieces of artwork to have the photos right there. In a lot of books, the pictures are all in an insert or stuck in the back, so if you want to reference one, you have to flip back and forth.<br /><br />So basically, this is a wordy book for wordy people. If you’re a book lover and reader like me I think you’ll enjoy it. There are some really priceless anecdotes in this lovely tome, but it does take some wading through some pretty hefty facts to get to them. For non-fiction lovers and book lovers alike, I think you’ll enjoy this book. But of course, it being ‘A’ history, rather than ‘the’ history of reading, you’ll be wanting more information on at least one of the topics covered in this book.<br /><br />3.5/ stars<br /><br />Book review blog: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://thebookgirlblog.wordpress.com/">http://thebookgirlblog.wordpress.com/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7752421874644591554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ANRIJrchQ09WFa1TgIwXLq5wPoixcdyPjdKLezDwdRbZQQXzByxeoMmz7uP1TUk1yntparNVm+pRL0yFFO7TAQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Johnson takes us to those times when the world seemed filled with mysterious forces, when scientists were dazzled by light, by electricity, and by the beating of the hearts they laid bare on the dissecting table. We see Galileo singing to mark time as he measures the pull of gravity, and Newton carefully inserting a needle behind his eye to learn how light causes vibrations in the retina. William Harvey ties a tourniquet around his arm and watches his arteries throb above and his veins bulge below, proving that blood circulates. Luigi Galvani sparks electrical currents in dissected frog legs, wondering at the twitching muscle fibers, and Ivan Pavlov makes his now-famous dogs salivate at ascending chord progressions.For all of them, diligence was rewarded. Frédéric Rouvillois reface istoria pasionantă a acestor cărţi cu destin neobişnuit, începând cu Coliba unchiului Tom (1853), trecând prin romane precum Pe aripile vântului sau seriile de romane poliţiste având eroi precum Hercule Poirot sau James Bond şi sfârşind cu romane apărute în zilele noastre, seria Harry Potter, Codul lui Da Vinci sau Alchimistul.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3602.Alberto_Manguel">About Alberto Manguel</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3602.Alberto_Manguel"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1227041892p3/3602.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3602.Alberto_Manguel">Alberto Manguel
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,321
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18326080131431392673">Alberto Manguel (born 1948 in Buenos Aires) is an Argentine-born writer, translator, and editor. He is the author of numerous non-fiction books such as The Dictionary of Imaginary Places (co-written with Gianni Guadalupi in 1980) and A History of Reading (1996) The Library at Night (2007) and Homer's Iliad and Odyssey: A Biography (2008), and novels such as News From a Foreign Country Came (1991).</span>
  <span id="freeText18326080131431392673" style="display:none">Alberto Manguel (born 1948 in Buenos Aires) is an Argentine-born writer, translator, and editor. He is the author of numerous non-fiction books such as The Dictionary of Imaginary Places (co-written with Gianni Guadalupi in 1980) and A History of Reading (1996) The Library at Night (2007) and Homer's Iliad and Odyssey: A Biography (2008), and novels such as News From a Foreign Country Came (1991). <br /><br />Manguel believes in the central importance of the book in societies of the written word where, in recent times, the intellectual act has lost most of its prestige. Libraries (the reservoirs of collective memory) should be our essential symbol, not banks. Humans can be defined as reading animals, come into the world to decipher it and themselves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18326080131431392673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
